Person v. State


No. 49A02-0106-CR-410


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA, SECOND DISTRICT


764 N.E.2d 743; 2002 Ind. App. LEXIS 380



March 12, 2002, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Petition to Transfer Denied August 13, 2002, Reported at: 2002 Ind. LEXIS 737.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM THE MARION SUPERIOR COURT. The Honorable Jane Barker, Magistrate. Cause No. 49G06-0004-CF-54030.



DISPOSITION: Trial court's judgment was affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury in the Marion Superior Court (Indiana) convicted defendant of unlawful possession of a firearm by a serious violent felon. The trial court had earlier denied defendant's motion to suppress evidence and for a bifurcated trial. The trial court sentenced defendant to a 15-year term of imprisonment. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was on the porch of a house with a history of narcotics activity. When an officer approached people outside the house, defendant looked at the officer, ran into the house, and out the back door. Defendant ran to a different house. The officer was given permission by the owner to enter the other house and, inside, found a gun. The appellate court found that with these facts, the officer had reasonable suspicion to believe defendant was involved in criminal activity. Defendant was not an overnight guest, but merely present in the other house with the owner's permission. Defendant had no reasonable expectation of privacy and lacked standing to challenge the search. The trial court did not err in denying defendant's motion for a bifurcated trial. Since the gun was in plain view and close to other items associated with defendant, there was sufficient evidence for the jury to infer that defendant was in constructive possession of the gun. The classification of the serious violent felon statute as a Class B felony was not unconstitutional. The trial court properly weighed the aggravating and mitigating circumstances and found that a 15-year sentence was appropriate.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Ind. Bell Tel. Co. v. Ind. Util. Regulatory Comm'n


No. 93A02-0107-EX-491


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA, SECOND DISTRICT


764 N.E.2d 734; 2002 Ind. App. LEXIS 381



March 12, 2002, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Application for Rehearing Denied May 2, 2002.
Rehearing denied by Ind. Bell Tel. Co. v. Ind. Util. Regulatory Comm., 2002 Ind. App. LEXIS 758 (Ind. Ct. App., May 2, 2002)
Transfer denied by Ind. Bell Tel. Co. v. Ind. Util. Regulatory Comm'n, 2002 Ind. LEXIS 1094 (Ind., Sept. 6, 2002)


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM THE INDIANA REGULATORY COMMISSION. The Honorable William D. McCarty, Chairman. Cause No. 41657 (EDR-1).



DISPOSITION: Decision of the Indiana Utility Regulatory Commission was affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a review of an administrative law judge's (ALJ) decision sought by appellants, telecommunications corporation and its affiliates, appellee Indiana Utility Regulatory Commission (IURC) held that the scope of the telecommunications corporation's operating support system (OSS) test was to be expanded to include a test of its digital subscriber lines' (DSL) resale offerings. The telecommunications corporation and its affiliates appealed.


OVERVIEW: The telecommunications corporation needed to comply with a 14-point list as a part of its request to enter the long distance telephone market. One point on the list was whether the telecommunications corporation's services were properly available for resale. The telecommunications corporation asked the IURC to review a test of its OSS to determine if it was in compliance with the list. The ALJ expanded the scope of the OSS testing to include DSL resale performance measurements. The telecommunications corporation argued that its DSL service was not a telecommunications service as contemplated by the Telecommunications Act of 1996 (Act). The appellate court held that an agency and a court needed to give effect to the expressed intent of Congress, which was that all telecommunications markets be open to competition. The purpose of the Act was to ensure that incumbent local exchange carriers did not use their control over local exchanges to squeeze out competitors. The possibility of anticompetitive pricing for DSL services was real. The IURC's order was proper. The appellate court also found that the IURC had proper jurisdiction and that its order did not violate due process.


OUTCOME: The order was affirmed.

Cheatham v. Pohle


No. 40A01-0010-CV-329


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA, FIRST DISTRICT


764 N.E.2d 272; 2002 Ind. App. LEXIS 338



February 28, 2002, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Rehearing Denied May 28, 2002, Reported at: 2002 Ind. App. LEXIS 1110.
Rehearing denied by Cheatham v. Pohle, 2002 Ind. App. LEXIS 1110 (Ind. Ct. App., May 28, 2002)
Transfer granted by Cheatham v. Pohle, 783 N.E.2d 695, 2002 Ind. LEXIS 1076 (Ind., 2002)
Later proceeding at Cheatham v. Pohle, 2003 Ind. LEXIS 440 (Ind., May 30, 2003)


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM THE JENNINGS SUPERIOR COURT. The Honorable Carl H. Taul, Special Judge. Cause No. 40D01-9803-CP-143.
Pohle v. Cheatham, 724 N.E.2d 655, 2000 Ind. App. LEXIS 250 (Ind. Ct. App., 2000)



DISPOSITION: Trial court's judgment was affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After a judgment on jury verdict in her favor, in the Jennings Superior Court (Indiana), plaintiff former wife, awarded a large amount in a tort action against defendant former husband, challenged the constitutionality of Ind. Code § 34-41-3-6, which mandated that 75 percent of her punitive damages award go to the state.


OVERVIEW:  Ind. Code § 34-51-3-6 required that clerks of court collect punitive damages awards, and that they be paid 25 percent to the successful plaintiff and 75 percent to the state's victim compensation fund. The former wife's award of damages for her former husband's violation of privacy and intentional infliction of extreme emotional distress for distributing nude pictures of her was designated half compensatory and half punitive. The appeals court held that there was no unconstitutional taking, because there was no right to punitive damages, and they could be abolished altogether. The statute did, however, violate the particular services clause of Ind. Const. art. I, § 21, because attorneys provided services that no one else could provide, citizens had a right to access, and when the extra effort needed to win an award of punitive damages went unrewarded because the client lacked the funds for an extra fee, the services had been provided without just compensation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment, and further held the statute unconstitutional.

Johnson v. Gupta


No. 64A03-0108-CV-259


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA, THIRD DISTRICT


762 N.E.2d 1280; 2002 Ind. App. LEXIS 191



February 19, 2002, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] APPEAL FROM THE PORTER SUPERIOR COURT. The Honorable Mary Harper, Judge. Cause No. 64D05-9501-CT-143.



DISPOSITION: Trial court's grant of defendants-appellees' motion for summary judgment was affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff patient appealed the judgment of the Porter Superior Court (Indiana) granting summary judgment in favor of defendant doctors in the patient's medical malpractice action.


OVERVIEW: In September 1990, the doctor performed a laser surgery on the patient. Almost immediately after surgery, the patient knew there was something wrong, because she was incontinent of stool. The patient's incontinence continued and she visited a second doctor in January 1992. The second doctor informed the patient that she had no rectal tone. In September 1994, a third doctor informed the patient that her rectal muscles had been irreparably severed during the laser surgery performed the doctor in 1990. The trial court properly found that the patient's case was barred the two-year statute of limitations under Ind. Code § 34-18-7-1. In January 1992, at the latest, the patient discovered facts that, in the exercise of reasonable diligence, should have led to the discovery of the malpractice. The two-year period began to run at that point. The patient did not file her proposed complaint until January 1995, well after the limitations period had expired.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Woodruff v. Klein


No. 12A02-0109-CV-610


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA, SECOND DISTRICT


762 N.E.2d 223; 2002 Ind. App. LEXIS 146



February 6, 2002, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Petition to Transfer Denied May 22, 2002, Reported at: 2002 Ind. LEXIS 595.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM THE CLINTON SUPERIOR COURT. The Honorable Joseph W. Carey, Special Judge. Cause No. 12D01-0009-MI-324.



DISPOSITION: Trial court's denial of petition for grandparent visitation was affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant grandmother filed an action against appellee father, pursuant to Indiana's Grandparent Visitation Statute, Ind. Code § 31-17-5-1 et seq., seeking a court order negating restrictions which her son-in-law imposed on visits between the grandmother and her grandson. The Clinton Superior Court (Indiana) denied the grandmother's petition, and the grandmother appealed.


OVERVIEW: A child's maternal grandmother filed an action against her son-in-law after he asked her to spend as many hours at his home visiting her grandson as her grandson spent visiting her at her home. The grandmother asked the court to order "as much visitation as possible and at a minimum weekend, weekday, and holiday visitation." Evidence showed that the child had lived in the grandmother's home during part of 1996 and that the grandmother visited the child and took care of him while his mother was alive. The child's mother died when he was six years old, however, and after her death the relationship which existed between the child's father and the child's maternal grandmother deteriorated. The appellate court held that the trial court had properly considered the lack of evidence that the child's father was an unfit parent, the presumption that fit parents act in the best interests of their children, and the fact that the father was not barring his mother-in-law from visiting her grandson under all circumstances, and had correctly determined that court-ordered visitation was not in the child's best interests.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment was affirmed.

 

722 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Hatcher v. State


No. 65A01-0104-CR-155


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA, FIRST DISTRICT


762 N.E.2d 170; 2002 Ind. App. LEXIS 132



January 31, 2002, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Petition to Transfer Denied March 26, 2002.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM THE POSEY CIRCUIT COURT. The Honorable James E. Redwine, Judge. Cause No. 65C01-0011-CF-0009.



DISPOSITION: Trial court's judgment was affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction of dealing in a schedule II controlled substance. He argued that the State was required to prove he manufactured methamphetamine for the use of another; and the Posey Circuit Court (Indiana) erred in overruling his motion for a mistrial, after a defense witness testified about the contents of a note that was not produced in response to a discovery motion.


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended that "manufacture," as defined in Ind. Code § 35-48-1-18, required the State prove the methamphetamine was not manufactured for his own use. The appellate court held that the legislature could not have intended to enact Ind. Code § 35-48-4-14.5 and allow one to be subjected to criminal liability for possession of the ingredients of methamphetamine, but to not be liable if the ingredients were used to manufacture the finished product. One could not commit the felony of manufacturing methamphetamine without possessing at least some hazardous substances; without using, pouring, and mixing those substances, or without applying heat. Thus, manufacturing methamphetamine, by its very nature, could not be committed without creating a substantial risk that someone would be killed. There was sufficient evidence that defendant was guilty of dealing in a schedule II controlled substance, even if the evidence did not support a finding he manufactured the methamphetamine for a purpose other than his own use. Because of the substantial evidence against defendant, the prejudicial effect of the testimony about the contents of a note written to his ex-wife was minimal.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

Austin v. Vanderburgh County Sheriff Merit Comm'n


No. 82A01-0101-CV-37


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA, FIRST DISTRICT


761 N.E.2d 875; 2002 Ind. App. LEXIS 23



January 16, 2002, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] APPEAL FROM THE VANDERBURGH CIRCUIT COURT. The Honorable Carl A. Heldt, Judge. Cause No. 82C01-9907-CP-271.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff sheriff's department officer appealed a Vanderburgh Circuit Court (Indiana) summary judgment dismissing for failure to state a claim his complaint that his due process rights were violated by the failure of defendant department and county merit commission to place his name on the eligibility list for promotion to sergeant.


OVERVIEW: An Indiana sheriff's department's internal personnel procedures provided that officers who met certain qualifications could be placed on an eligibility list for promotion to sergeant. The procedures made clear, however, that any actual promotions were made by the county merit commission, in the exercise of its discretion. An officer barely achieved the requisite examination score for promotion and met the other requirements, but the merit commission decided his name should be kept off the eligibility list because of his extensive disciplinary history. The appeals court held that the officer failed to show violation of a protected property interest within the meaning of the due process requirements of U.S. Const. amend. XIV and Ind. Const. art. I, § 12, because the decision to promote was entirely discretionary. Even if the officer had been placed on the eligibility list, he would have had no protected right to be promoted.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the summary judgment.

Van Winkle v. Nash


No. 49A05-0103-CV-109


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA, FIFTH DISTRICT


761 N.E.2d 856; 2002 Ind. App. LEXIS 5



January 10, 2002, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] APPEAL FROM THE MARION COUNTY SUPERIOR COURT. Cause No. 49D02-9703-CT-0330. The Honorable Kenneth H. Johnson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part, reversed in part and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellees, injured person and her husband, sued appellant negligent motorist seeking damages for injuries arising from a traffic accident. A jury in the Marion County Superior Court (Indiana) rendered a verdict in favor of the injured person in the amount of $ 82,000. The trial court then awarded costs and prejudgment interest on the judgment amount. After the trial court denied her motion to correct errors, the negligent motorist appealed.


OVERVIEW: Prior to trial, the injured person and her husband had made a written settlement demand for $ 25,000 pursuant to the Tort Prejudgment Interest Statute, Ind. Code §§ 34-51-4-1 to -9. The demand was rejected. The negligent motorist claimed that the Tort Prejudgment Interest Statute was unconstitutionally vague and violated her due process rights. In the alternative, the negligent motorist claimed that the trial court erred in its award of prejudgment interest under the statute. The negligent motorist had not raised this issue to the trial court before filing the motion to correct errors, and the appellate court ruled that this issue was therefore waived on appeal. The Tort Prejudgment Interest Statute contained no exception providing that interest should not be awarded where there were disputed issues. The trial court did not abuse its discretion in awarding prejudgment interest. However, the term "costs" was strictly interpreted to include only filing fees and statutory witness fees and the trial court erred in including in the costs award expenses for deposition transcription, medical records acquisition, photograph and diagram exhibits, and photocopying.


OUTCOME: The award of prejudgment interest was affirmed. The costs award was reversed and the case was remanded for a cost determination consistent with the opinion. It was ordered that the cost determination would also include the costs for appeal, with 70 percent to be taxed to the negligent motorist and 30 percent to be taxed to the injured person and his wife.

 

725 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Glover v. State


No. 49A02-0106-CR-384


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA, SECOND DISTRICT


760 N.E.2d 1120; 2002 Ind. App. LEXIS 4



January 9, 2002, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Petition to Transfer Denied March 15, 2002, Reported at: 2002 Ind. LEXIS 218.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM THE MARION SUPERIOR COURT. The Honorable Tanya Walton Pratt, Judge. Cause No. 49G01-9910-CF-172478.



DISPOSITION: Trial court's judgment was affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury in the Marion Superior Court (Indiana) convicted defendant of rape. Prior to trial, and again at trial, defendant moved to dismiss the case, which the trial court denied. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: The victim had passed out while drinking, and while she was unconscious, defendant had sex with her. Defendant argued that he was entitled to dismissal since the statute with which he was charged, Ind. Code § 35-42-4-1(a)(2) was unconstitutionally vague in that the term "unaware" was undefined. The appellate court held that the definition of the term "unaware" was not aware, lacking knowledge or acquaintance, unconscious. Ind. Code § 35-42-4-1(a)(2) required that the victim be "unaware" that the sex was occurring. Generally, if a man had intercourse with a woman while she was asleep, he was guilty of rape since the act was without her consent. The language of the statute was adequate to inform an individual of ordinary intelligence that sexual intercourse with an individual who had lost consciousness due to inebriation was proscribed. The evidence was sufficient to support the conviction. The State provided logical non-discriminatory reasons for peremptory juror strikes, as its responses, as a whole, did not disclose inherent discriminatory intent. Defendant demonstrated no reversible error in regard to the State's peremptory strikes of three black women.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

726 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Cooper v. State


No. 48A02-0104-CR-229


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA, SECOND DISTRICT


760 N.E.2d 660; 2001 Ind. App. LEXIS 2228



December 31, 2001, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Petition to Transfer Denied March 21, 2002, Reported at: 2002 Ind. LEXIS 232.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM THE MADISON SUPERIOR COURT. The Honorable Dennis D. Carroll, Judge. Cause No. 48D01-0009-CF-343.



DISPOSITION: Trial court's judgment was affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment from the Madison Superior Court (Indiana) that convicted him of nonsupport of a dependent child.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, defendant presented four issues: (1) whether Ind. Code § 35-46-1-5 was unconstitutionally vague; (2) whether he could have asserted his prior incarcerations as an affirmative defense to his inability to pay; (3) whether the burden of establishing the affirmative defense of inability to pay violated his constitutional right to due process; and (4) whether there was sufficient evidence to support his conviction. Section 35-46-1-5 unambiguously informed defendant that criminal penalties could have attached to his failure to support his children and was not unconstitutionally vague. While defendant was able to offer evidence of his incarceration as an affirmative defense, his incarceration was not an absolute bar to his conviction for nonsupport of a dependant child as a Class C felony. Because defendant had the burden of proving his affirmative defense of inability to pay, it was his prerogative to assess whether the probative value of his criminal history was substantially outweighed by its prejudicial nature. Defendant was not "forced" to reveal his criminal background. The evidence was sufficient to support defendant's conviction.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

727 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Robles v. State


No. 50A04-0104-CR-162


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA, FOURTH DISTRICT


758 N.E.2d 581; 2001 Ind. App. LEXIS 1986



November 21, 2001, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Petition to Transfer Denied March 21, 2002, Reported at: 2002 Ind. LEXIS 241.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM THE MARSHALL CIRCUIT COURT. Cause No. 50C01-0006-CF-6. The Honorable Michael D. Cook, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Trial court convictions of defendant affirmed in part and reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury in the Marshall Circuit Court (Indiana) convicted defendant of possession of an altered handgun as a Class C felony, Ind. Code §§ 35-47-2-18, 35-47-2-23(b), and criminal gang activity, a Class D felony, Ind. Code § 35-45-9-3. Defendant appealed, arguing that there was insufficient evidence to support the convictions.


OVERVIEW: When police responded to reports of gunshots, they found a handgun in the area on which the serial number had been obliterated. An officer testified that the area where the serial number had been was "very shiny." Other witnesses testified that they had seen defendant with the gun on the night of the arrest and at his birthday party a week earlier. The appellate court held that for a conviction of possession of an altered handgun, the State was required to prove that defendant knew that the serial number had been altered. Knowledge could be proved by circumstantial evidence. The appellate court held that based on the officer's testimony and the fact that defendant possessed the gun for over a week and had ample opportunity to discover that the serial number had been removed, the State provided adequate evidence to support the conviction. However, the appellate court held that the State failed to establish a nexus between defendant's gang membership and the charged crimes. Thus, the State failed to present probative evidence from which a reasonable jury could have found defendant guilty of criminal gang activity beyond a reasonable doubt.


OUTCOME: The conviction for possession of an altered handgun was affirmed, but the conviction for criminal gang activity was reversed.

 

728 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Shepler v. State


No. 43A03-0104-CR-117


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA, THIRD DISTRICT


758 N.E.2d 966; 2001 Ind. App. LEXIS 1979



November 20, 2001, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Transfer Denied February 7, 2002, Reported at: 2002 Ind. LEXIS 116.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM THE KOSCIUSKO SUPERIOR COURT. The Honorable James C. Jarrette, Judge. Cause No. 43D02-9906-CM-991.



DISPOSITION: Defendant-appellant's conviction was affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Following a bench trial in the Kosciusko Superior Court (Indiana), defendant was convicted of operating a vehicle with a Schedule I or II controlled substance or its metabolite in his body. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that Ind. Code § 9-30-5-1 violated equal protection as there was no threshold level of impairment needed for those with controlled substances in their blood as was required of those with alcohol in their blood. The appellate court found that it was impossible to determine the amount of marijuana or cocaine in the body and there was no agreement as to the quantity of a controlled substance needed to cause impairment. Thus, it was reasonable to distinguish between alcohol and controlled substances and to ban driving with any level of controlled substance in the body. Defendant also argued that the statute violated substantive due process, but banning those with any level of controlled substances in their body from driving was not without a rational basis. The disparate treatment reasonably distinguished between those with alcohol from those with controlled substances in their blood, so the statute did not confer an improper privilege. As the State was required to prove that defendant operated a vehicle with the drugs in his body, it was his driving, and not his status as a drug user, that was being punished. Ind. Code § 9-30-5-1 was not unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

729 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Rogers v. Mendel


No. 82A04-0103-CV-84


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA, FOURTH DISTRICT


758 N.E.2d 946; 2001 Ind. App. LEXIS 1962



November 16, 2001, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Rehearing Denied January 14, 2002. Petition to Transfer Denied May 17, 2002, Reported at: 2002 Ind. LEXIS 589.


PRIOR HISTORY: PERMISSIVE INTERLOCUTORY APPEAL FROM THE VANDERBURGH CIRCUIT COURT. The Honorable Carl A. Heldt, Judge. Cause No. 82C01-0005-CT-271.



DISPOSITION: Trial court's denial of plaintiff-appellant's motion for summary judgment was reversed and cause was remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee representative filed a proposed medical malpractice complaint against appellant doctor. A decision in favor of the representative was issued. The representative brought suit against the doctor. The doctor moved for summary judgment. The Vanderburgh Circuit Court (Indiana) denied the doctor's motion. The doctor filed an interlocutory appeal.


OVERVIEW: The doctor argued that he was entitled to summary judgment because the representative failed to file the complaint within the two-year, occurrence-based limitations period for medical malpractice suits. The court of appeals agreed that the facts material to the statute of limitations issue were not in dispute. The court noted that the applicable limitations period began at the latest when the doctor failed to inform the deceased of the test results by the time he released her from his care on January 4, 1994. After receiving the deceased's diagnosis of metastatic endometrial cancer in March, 1995, the representative had 10 months of the occurrence-based two-year statute of limitations remaining during which he should have filed his complaint. The court noted that 10 months within which to initiate an action was not an eve of midnight discovery, but rather was a timeframe within which filing the complaint would be reasonably possible.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed and the case was remanded with instructions to enter summary judgment in favor of the doctor.

 

730 of 1119 DOCUMENTS



Shah v. Harris


No. 82A01-0103-CV-111


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA, FIRST DISTRICT


758 N.E.2d 953; 2001 Ind. App. LEXIS 1977



November 16, 2001, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Rehearing Denied January 14, 2002. Petition to Transfer Denied May 17, 2002, Reported at: 2002 Ind. LEXIS 445.


PRIOR HISTORY: PERMISSIVE INTERLOCUTORY APPEAL FROM THE VANDERBURGH CIRCUIT COURT. The Honorable Carl A. Heldt, Judge. Cause No. 82C01-0007-CT-357.



DISPOSITION: Trial court's denial of plaintiff-appellant's motion for summary judgment was affirmed, cause was remanded for entry of summary judgment in favor of defendants-appellees.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff patient and his wife filed suit against defendant doctor, alleging he misdiagnosed a vitamin B-12 deficiency as multiple sclerosis. The doctor filed for summary judgment, alleging the claims were barred by the two-year statute of limitations, Ind. Code § 34-18-7-1(b) (1998). The Vanderburgh Circuit Court (Indiana) denied the motion, finding a genuine issue of material fact existed, and certified its order for interlocutory appeal.


OVERVIEW: The patient was diagnosed with a vitamin B-12 deficiency about seven years after the original, erroneous diagnosis of multiple sclerosis. Defendant physician had relocated out of the area about two years after the erroneous diagnosis. The complaint was filed one week less than two years after the date of the second diagnosis. In upholding the trial court's denial of the doctor's motion for summary judgment, the appellate court found that the occurrence-based statute of limitations, Ind. Code § 34-18-7-1(b) (1998), was unconstitutional as applied to plaintiffs who could not have discovered the injury with reasonable diligence within two years of the alleged misconduct. Since the facts of the instant case were undisputed, and there was no evidence or inference that the patient reasonably could have or should have discovered the misdiagnosis, the limitation period did not begin to run until he obtained the information regarding the vitamin deficiency. Since the complaint was filed within two years thereafter, the denial of the doctor's motion for summary judgment was proper. Additionally, with undisputed facts, the patient was entitled to summary judgment on the issue.


OUTCOME: The trial court's order denying the doctor's motion for summary judgment was affirmed and the case was remanded for entry of summary judgment in favor of the patient and his wife on the issue of the applicable limitations period.

Hornbostel v. State


No. 82A01-0103-CR-92


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA, FIRST DISTRICT

PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] APPEAL FROM THE VANDERBURGH SUPERIOR COURT. Cause No. 82D02-0008-CF-00764. The Honorable Robert J. Pigman, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Vanderburgh Superior Court (Indiana), which convicted him of murder and sentenced him with a habitual offender enhancement. Defendant was convicted of theft and auto theft.


OVERVIEW: Defendant met the victim at a gay bar and went home with him because he needed a place to sleep. After resisting the victim's sexual advances, defendant choked the victim to death. Defendant then took some of the victim's electronic equipment and his vehicle. The appellate court held that the State provided the trial court with sufficient evidence to prove that defendant's convictions in another state were felonies. Defendant was properly adjudged a habitual offender. The instruction on voluntary intoxication did not misstate the law. The evidence was sufficient to support the murder conviction. The error in allowing a detective to testify as to whether he believed various assertions made by defendant was not prejudicial. The trial court properly considered defendant killing the victim with his bare hands as part of the nature and circumstances of the crime committed. Drinking was not to be considered as a mitigating circumstances.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Cowart v. State


No. 49A02-0103-CR-124


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA, SECOND DISTRICT


756 N.E.2d 581; 2001 Ind. App. LEXIS 1804



October 18, 2001, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Petition to Transfer Denied January 17, 2002, Reported at: 2002 Ind. LEXIS 59.
Transfer denied by Cowart v. State, 774 N.E.2d 505, 2002 Ind. LEXIS 59 (Ind., 2002)
Decision reached on appeal by Cowart v. State, 783 N.E.2d 1283, 2003 Ind. App. LEXIS 257 (Ind. Ct. App., 2003)
Post-conviction proceeding at, Decision reached on appeal by Cowart v. State, 873 N.E.2d 1140, 2007 Ind. App. LEXIS 2221 (Ind. Ct. App., 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM THE MARION SUPERIOR COURT. CRIMINAL DIVISION 3. The Honorable Cale Bradford, Judge. Cause No. 49G03-0006-CF-90308.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the denial of his motion to dismiss two counts of child molestation by the Marion Superior Court (Indiana), claiming the age classifications in Ind. Code § 35-42-4-3 violated Ind. Const. art. I, § 23, and U.S. Const. amend. XIV.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with two counts of molesting a five-year old child. His motion to dismiss was denied. His motion to certify an order for interlocutory appeal was granted. The appellate court held that where a class of persons received a privilege or immunity not equally provided to others, the classification had to be reasonably related to distinguishing characteristics of the group. Persons over 21 were presumed to be mature and understand the consequences of their actions. Therefore, they could be held to a different standard than those younger than 21. Because all persons over 21 were treated similarly, there were no constitutional violations.


OUTCOME: The motion to dismiss was affirmed.

Lark v. State


No. 48A02-0011-CR-723


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA, SECOND DISTRICT


755 N.E.2d 1153; 2001 Ind. App. LEXIS 1703



October 2, 2001, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] APPEAL FROM THE MADISON COUNTY COURT. Cause No. 48E01-9708-DF-243. The Honorable David W. Hopper, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Madison County Court (Indiana) of resisting law enforcement, driving while suspended, and possession of marijuana. At trial, the trial court denied defendant's motion to suppress. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Police officers saw defendant's car parked in the middle of a road, blocking traffic, with a man leaning into the window on the passenger's side of the car. The unidentified man walked away when he saw the police approaching. Before the officers could initiate a traffic stop, defendant drove away. By then, defendant's car had been parked in the street for at least 15 seconds. The officers followed defendant for several blocks and then executed a traffic stop. As one officer approached defendant's car, he smelled marijuana. During a pat down search, defendant fled on foot. After arresting defendant, the officers searched defendant's car and found a bag of marijuana. The appellate court held that police could stop a vehicle when an officer observed a minor traffic violation. For purposes of the validity of a vehicle stop following a traffic infraction, it was of no significance that the stop was executed several blocks from the scene of the infraction. The officer's suspicion of illegal activity that was aroused by the odor of marijuana provided a reasonable suspicion that the vehicle contained narcotics, thus permitting a search of the vehicle after the traffic stop.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

736 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


In re G.B.


No. 15A05-0101-JV-34


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA, FIFTH DISTRICT


754 N.E.2d 1027; 2001 Ind. App. LEXIS 1598



September 12, 2001, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] APPEAL FROM THE DEARBORN CIRCUIT COURT. Cause No. 15C01-0009-JC-17. The Honorable James D. Humphrey, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee department of family and children filed a child in need of services petition regarding the children of appellants parents. The Dearborn Circuit Court (Indiana), following hearings, found that reasonable efforts to reunify the child with the parents was not required. The parents appealed.


OVERVIEW: At the hearings, evidence was presented that a court had terminated the parents' relationship with three other children in earlier proceedings. Ind. Code § 31-34-21-5.6 provided that reasonable efforts to reunify a child with the parents were not required if the rights of the parents with respect to a child had been involuntarily terminated by a court in another case. The parents argued that this infringed on their fundamental right to family integrity and violated due process. The appellate court held that there was a fundamental right to family integrity, and as Ind. Code § 31-34-21-5.6 burdened the exercise of that right, it received the strictest scrutiny. The fundamental right to family integrity was not unlimited as the state had a compelling interest in protecting the welfare of children. With some parents the risk of recidivism was a real concern, and when another child of that same parent was adjudged dependent, it was not unreasonable to assume that reunification efforts would be unsuccessful. Ind. Code § 31-34-21-5.6 served a compelling state interest and was narrowly tailored to serve that interest; thus it did not violate substantive due process.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Parker v. State


No. 10A01-0010-CR-344


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA, FIRST DISTRICT


754 N.E.2d 614; 2001 Ind. App. LEXIS 1502



August 31, 2001, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] APPEAL FROM THE CLARK SUPERIOR COURT. Cause No. 10D01-9912-CF-79. The Honorable Jerome F. Jacobi, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of robbery by a jury in the Clark Superior Court (Indiana). Prior to trial, defendant's motion to suppress his statement to police was denied. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was a juvenile when he and two other men robbed a pawnshop. The owner of the pawnshop was shot by one of the other men during the robbery. After his mother arrived at the police station, and she and the defendant signed a Miranda advisement and waiver form, defendant confessed. Defendant argued on appeal that admission of his confession by the trial court was error. The appellate court reviewed the circumstances of the statement and found that the trial court did not err in finding that defendant's statement was given voluntarily. Defendant also challenged the constitutionality of Ind. Code § 35-50-2-11, a sentencing enhancement statute based on use of a firearm during the robbery. The application of the statute did not offend due process under the Jones and Apprendi standard. The charging information alleged that defendant committed the robbery while armed with a handgun, and all of the bases for the felony conviction arose out of the use of the gun during the robbery, so the jury must have found that a gun was used. The fact that defendant himself was not the actual shooter was irrelevant, because defendant's actions had aided in the commission of the robbery.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Schenkel v. Gaunt (In re J.D.G.)


No. 02A03-0103-CV-67


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA, THIRD DISTRICT


756 N.E.2d 509; 2001 Ind. App. LEXIS 1474



August 29, 2001, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] APPEAL FROM THE ALLEN SUPERIOR COURT. The Honorable Stephen M. Sims, Judge. Cause No. 02D07-9905-DR-248.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant grandparents challenged the judgment of the Allen Superior Court (Indiana) which denied their petition for grandparent visitation with their biological grandson and the adoptive son of appellee adoptive father.


OVERVIEW: The biological mother and adoptive father lived together. Following the biological mother's death, the adoptive father initiated paternity proceedings to establish paternity of the child. However, DNA test results excluded the adoptive father as the biological father of the child. Later, the adoptive father adopted the child. The grandparents requested post-adoption grandparent visitation. The trial court found that the grandparents lacked standing to seek post-adoption visitation rights. On appeal, the grandparents argued the trial court erred in finding the grandparents lacked standing to pursue a claim under the Indiana Grandparent Visitation Statute, Ind. Code § 31-17-5-1 et seq. Specifically, the grandparents contended that the purpose and intent of the Grandparent Visitation Statute would be thwarted if they were denied a continuing relationship with the child. The appellate court concluded that inasmuch as the adoptive father was not a step-parent or a biological relative specified in Ind. Code § 31-17-5-9, the visitation rights awarded the grandparents during the adoptive father's guardianship of the child did not survive his adoption of the child.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the judgment because the trial court correctly found the grandparents lacked standing to obtain post-adoption visitation rights.

Garner v. State


No. 31A01-0012-CR-437


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA, FIRST DISTRICT


754 N.E.2d 984; 2001 Ind. App. LEXIS 1475



August 29, 2001, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Transfer granted by, Vacated by: Garner v. State, 2002 Ind. LEXIS 162 (Ind. Feb. 22, 2002). Petition for Rehearing Denied October 15, 2001, Reported at: 2001 Ind. App. LEXIS 1846.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM THE HARRISON SUPERIOR COURT. Cause No. 31D01-0002-CF-114. The Honorable Roger D. Davis, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of two counts of child molesting by a jury in the Harrison Superior Court (Indiana) and was sentenced. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the information was vague and failed to inform him adequately of the charges against him. The charging information did not specify the exact dates of the alleged crimes, but it narrowed the range of dates to five months. The appellate court held that because time was not the essence of the offense of child molesting, it was sufficient to allege time specifically enough to show that the offense was committed within the period of limitations. The crimes were adequately charged to allow defendant to prepare a defense. Because the information was sufficient, defendant was not prejudiced by his counsel's failure to bring a motion to dismiss and his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel was unfounded. Evidence of other incidents of sexual conduct between defendant and the child victim and an incident involving both the victim and her sister was properly admitted as direct evidence that defendant committed the crimes and thus was not evidence offered to show defendant's bad character. Depositions of absent witnesses were properly admitted where defendant had the opportunity to cross-examine. Finally, the sentence was proper under the facts of the case.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

740 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Caudle v. State


No. 49A02-0008-CR-546


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA, SECOND DISTRICT


754 N.E.2d 33; 2001 Ind. App. LEXIS 1436



August 21, 2001, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] APPEAL FROM THE MARION SUPERIOR COURT. Cause No. 49G20-9907-CF-125628. The Honorable Michael Jensen, Judge.

Original Opinion of June 4, 2001, Reported at: 2001 Ind. App. LEXIS 898.



DISPOSITION: We grant Caudle's petition for rehearing and affirm our earlier decision.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Marion Superior Court (Indiana) had denied defendant's motion to suppress evidence. The Indiana Court of Appeals had earlier affirmed the trial court's denial. Defendant petitioned for rehearing.


OVERVIEW: A detective executed a search warrant after the probable cause supporting the warrant had allegedly dissipated. In its earlier opinion affirming the trial court' denial of the motion to dismiss, the appellate court relied on the good faith exception to the exclusionary rule. After the search in question, the appellate court had held that the execution of a warrant within the 10 day period for execution of warrants under Ind. Code Ann. § 35-33-5-7 could be unconstitutional if the probable cause supporting the warrant had dissipated before the search. Prior to that decision, however, the police officer could rely on the statute when executing the search unless the statute was "clearly unconstitutional." The good faith exception should have applied when an officer relied in good faith on the constitutionality of a statute that was later determined to be unconstitutional, because an officer could not be expected to question the judgment of the legislature that passed the law. Police officers were not expected to understand the intricacies of the constitution like attorneys should have. The good faith exception applied in the case.


OUTCOME: The petition for rehearing was granted and the earlier decision was affirmed.

Black v. ACandS, Inc.


No. 45A04-9912-CV-565


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA, FOURTH DISTRICT


752 N.E.2d 148; 2001 Ind. App. LEXIS 1231



July 23, 2001, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Counsel Corrected July 25, 2001.
Rehearing denied by Black v. A.C. & S., Inc., 2001 Ind. App. LEXIS 1929 (Ind. Ct. App., Nov. 7, 2001)
Superseded by, Remanded by Black v. A.C.& S., 2003 Ind. LEXIS 266 (Ind., Mar. 25, 2003)
Transfer granted by Black v. A.C.&S., Inc., 2003 Ind. LEXIS 282 (Ind., Mar. 25, 2003)


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM THE LAKE COUNTY SUPERIOR COURT. Cause No. 45D01-9807-CT-521. The Honorable Jeffrey J. Dywan, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part, reversed in part, and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff widow appealed the judgment of the Superior Court, Lake County (Indiana) which granted defendant insulation and refrigeration companies' motion for summary judgment in an action to recover for the wrongful death of the widow's husband.


OVERVIEW: Decedent was exposed to asbestos over a 27 year period of his employment which ultimately led to his death. Decedent's widow filed suit against an insulation company and multiple other defendants. All defendants filed for summary judgment which the trial court granted. The widow did not oppose two of the motions. However, the remaining refractory companies and insulation companies, which filed for summary judgment on the basis that the widow's claims were filed outside of the 10-year products liability statute of repose codified at Ind. Code § 34-20-3-1, and that the widow failed to present sufficient evidence that the decedent was exposed to their asbestos-containing products. The trial court granted those motions, and the widow appealed. The appellate court found that the statutory construction urged by the defendants was inconsistent with other provisions of the product liability act. The appellate court found, therefore, that summary judgment on the ground that none of the companies mined commercial asbestos was improper. However, for those refractory and insulation companies which moved on the basis of sufficiency of the evidence, the trial court did not err.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court which granted summary judgment on the basis of the statute of limitation was reversed and remanded. That portion of the judgment which granted summary judgment on the basis of lack of evidence was affirmed.

City of South Bend v. Kimsey


No. 71A03-0101-CV-13


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA, THIRD DISTRICT


751 N.E.2d 805; 2001 Ind. App. LEXIS 1195



July 17, 2001, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Transfer granted by, Vacated by: City of S. Bend v. Kimsey, 2002 Ind. LEXIS 203 (Ind. Mar. 15, 2002).


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM THE ST. JOSEPH SUPERIOR COURT. Cause No. 71D03-9609-CP-909. The Honorable William C. Whitman, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant city appealed the order of the St. Joseph Superior Court (Indiana) denying annexation of land by the city in a remonstrance action commenced by plaintiffs landowners.


OVERVIEW: The city sought to annex part of a county with a population between 200,000 and 300,000. Ind. Code § 36-4-3-13(e) provided that counties of such a size were not to be annexed. The trial court prohibited the city's attempted to annex the land in question. The city argued that § 36-4-3-13(e) was not a general law as required by Ind. Const. art. IV, § 23. The challenged statute was applicable to any county that, because of increased or decreased population growth, fell within the prescribed limits. The statute prescribed neither a restrictive population range nor mandatory cut-off time for qualification. Thus, it was not predestined to apply to only one county. It was susceptible of uniform application to any county in the State meeting the population criteria. It was general rather than special legislation. The city failed to prove that Ind. Code § 36-4-3-13(e) was unreasonable.


OUTCOME: The order was affirmed.

 

743 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Crafton v. Gibson


No. 40A04-0011-CV-490


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA, FOURTH DISTRICT


752 N.E.2d 78; 2001 Ind. App. LEXIS 1184



July 11, 2001, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] APPEAL FROM THE JENNINGS CIRCUIT COURT. Cause No. 40C01-9709-DR-226. The Honorable Jon W. Webster, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded with instructions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mother sought relief, under Ind. R. Trial P. 60(B)(7), from a judgment by the Jennings Circuit Court (Indiana) which granted grandparent visitation to appellee grandmother, pursuant to Indiana's Grandparent Visitation Act. Ind. Code § 31-17-5-1. Following denial of her motion for relief from the judgment, the mother appealed.


OVERVIEW: The mother, in presenting a motion for relief from a judgment which granted the grandmother visitation, cited a case which was decided after the entry of the judgment. She argued the original order was no longer equitable and the trial court had improperly failed to apply the presumption that the mother's decision to limit or deny visitation was in her child's best interests. The appellate court stated the Due Process Clause of the Fourteenth Amendment protected the fundamental right of parents to make decisions concerning the care, custody, and control of their children. It also held there was a presumption that fit parents acted in the best interests of their children. Applying a rational basis test, the constitutionality of the Indiana Grandparent Visitation Act was upheld. A new hearing on the grandparent visitation request was ordered to properly develop the record and present evidence, with the burden upon the grandmother to rebut the presumption that the mother was acting in the best interests of her child in attempting to limit or deny visitation.


OUTCOME: The denial of the mother's motion was reversed and remanded to the trial court for a new hearing on the grandmother's petition for grandparent visitation. Instructions were given to apply special weight to the mother's decision regarding the grandmother's request for visitation, and some weight to any voluntary offer of visitation made by the mother, if the trial court concluded that such an offer was made.

Hopster v. Burgeson


No. 19A01-0008-CV-291


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA, FIRST DISTRICT


750 N.E.2d 841; 2001 Ind. App. LEXIS 1084



June 21, 2001, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] APPEAL FROM THE DUBOIS CIRCUIT COURT. Cause No. 19C01-9611-MI-243. The Honorable William E. Weikert, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part, reversed and remanded in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff husband filed a wrongful death and malpractice action against defendants, doctors, stemming from the death of the husband's wife. The Dubois Circuit Court (Indiana) granted summary judgment in favor of the doctors, finding that the action was not commenced with the two-year statute of limitations provided by Ind. Code § 34-18-7-1(b). The husband appealed the decision.


OVERVIEW: The wife died due to a rupture of her ascending aorta. The husband asserted that the doctors in question were negligent because the care they rendered fell below the accepted standard of care and resulted in the misdiagnosis of the wife's condition, and that this misdiagnosis significantly contributed to the wife's failure to obtain appropriate medical care. The appellate court held that the trial court properly determined that the husband's actions against all except one doctor were barred by the statute of limitations. The appellate court found that Ind. Code § 34-18-7-1(b) was not unconstitutional in the instant case, because the statute of limitations did not shorten the window of time so unreasonably that it was impractical for the husband to file the claims. However, the appellate court held that the husband presented genuine issues of fact as to whether the doctrine of fraudulent concealment tolled the statute of limitations as to one doctor. The husband presented facts indicating that the wife's patient-physician relationship with the doctor continued until the wife's death, and the statute of limitations ran from the time the patient-physician relationship ended.


OUTCOME: The grants of summary judgment in favor of all except one doctor were affirmed. The grant of summary judgment against the remaining doctor was reversed and remanded.

Harvey v. State


No. 10A01-0012-CR-436


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA, FIRST DISTRICT


751 N.E.2d 254; 2001 Ind. App. LEXIS 947



June 7, 2001, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] APPEAL FROM THE CLARK SUPERIOR COURT. Cause No. 10D02-9904-CF-55. The Honorable Cecile Blau, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with a cocaine offense and was granted bond on condition that she submit to drug testing while on bond. After posting bond, defendant refused to submit to a drug test that she was ordered to take immediately following a final pre-trial hearing. Later, upon the State's motion, the Clark Superior Court, Indiana, revoked defendant's bond, and ordered a jail sentence for contempt. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, defendant argued that revoking her bond was an abuse of discretion absent reasonable suspicion of drug use to justify ordering the drug test, and that the trial judge showed bias in ordering the drug test. The State, in what the appellate court construed as cross-appeal issues, argued that defendant waived consideration of her appeal because the bond revocation order was interlocutory and defendant did not comply with the procedure for an interlocutory appeal, and that defendant waived consideration of her bond revocation claim by failing to provide a transcript of a bond reduction hearing. Because the revocation of defendant's bond was severable from the adjudication of the charge against her and because the trial court declared the bond revocation to be a final appealable order, the appellate court chose to address the appeal and further held that the missing transcript was not necessary to the issues on appeal. The appellate court found no abuse of discretion and held that defendant's failure to object when the trial court imposed random drug testing as a bond condition waived later objections to such testing and that defendant failed to prove actual bias.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the trial court's judgment.

Boczar v. Meridian St. Found.


No. 49A02-0101-CV-24


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA, SECOND DISTRICT

June 5, 2001, Filed

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, a real estate preservation commission and people who owned homes in the preservation area, sued defendant's, husband and wife. Caudle v. State

DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction for dealing in cocaine by the Marion Superior Court (Indiana), claiming the affidavit provided insufficient probable cause to justify issuance of the search warrant and the warrant was stale when executed.


OVERVIEW: A search warrant was issued based upon police officer's surveillance and evidence from a confidential informant. The warrant was executed nine days later. Cocaine and firearms were confiscated. Defendant was convicted of cocaine dealing. On appeal, the appellate court upheld the warrant. The informant's reliability was not an issue because the warrant was not based upon the informant's statements, but on the officer's own observations. The officer executed the warrant in good faith even though there were no other controlled buys. Subsequent sales were reported. Surveillance continued and other controlled buys were attempted. Finally, defendant offered to sell cocaine to informant three days prior to execution.


OUTCOME: The conviction was affirmed.

Baker v. State


No. 49A02-0010-CR-686


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA, SECOND DISTRICT


747 N.E.2d 633; 2001 Ind. App. LEXIS 755



May 9, 2001, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Appellant's Transfer Denied August 9, 2001, Reported at: 2001 Ind. LEXIS 700.
Transfer denied by Baker v. State, 761 N.E.2d 414, 2001 Ind. LEXIS 700 (Ind., 2001)
Subsequent appeal at Baker v. State, 2012 Ind. App. Unpub. LEXIS 16 (Ind. Ct. App., Jan. 9, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM THE MARION SUPERIOR COURT. Cause No. 49G02-0001-CF-2522. The Honorable Robyn L. Moberly, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his convictions, in Marion Superior Court, of aggravated battery and weapon possession by a serious violent offender, arguing that prosecutorial misconduct denied him a fair trial and that Ind. Code § 35-47-4-5 violated his constitutional guarantees of the right to bear arms and equal protection.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued he was denied a fair trial when a prosecutor stated in closing argument that he could not argue self-defense in response to charges of murder and aggravated battery. The reviewing court held, first, that defendant waived the issue because he failed to move for admonishment or mistrial at the time. Furthermore, there was no error, because the trial court accurately instructed the jury on the law of self-defense. Ind. Code § 35-47-4-5, which prohibited serious violent felons from knowingly or intentionally possessing weapons, did not violate Indiana's privileges or immunities clause, Ind. Const. art. I, § 23, or a public policy favoring reformation of felons, or the right to bear arms guarantee of Ind. Const. art. I, § 32, or the equal protection guarantee of U.S. Const. amend. XIV. Defendant was not a member of a protected class, and the law had a rational relationship to preventing violence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed. Although defendant waived misconduct issue, there was no error anyway, because jurors were properly instructed. The challenged statute had a rational basis, in protecting the community against those with a strong likelihood of engaging in violence again.

Wilcox v. State


No. 49A02-0008-CR-537


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA, SECOND DISTRICT


748 N.E.2d 906; 2001 Ind. App. LEXIS 707



April 30, 2001, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Petition to Transfer Denied July 19, 2001.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM THE MARION SUPERIOR COURT. Cause No. 49F17-0003-CM-51850. The Honorable Ruth Reichard, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed an interlocutory appeal challenging the Marion Superior Court's (Indiana) denial of her motion to dismiss the battery and invasion of privacy charges against her. She claimed that her prosecution for those charges subjected her to double jeopardy because the same conduct that supported the revocation of her bail formed the basis for the charges.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was released on bail prior to her trial on unrelated charges on condition that she have no contact with her ex-boyfriend's girlfriend and that she refrain from abusing or threatening her ex-boyfriend. Defendant's bail was revoked after she violated both conditions by attacking the ex-boyfriend and arguing with the girlfriend. The same conduct led to the battery and invasion of privacy charges that she sought to dismiss. Her bond was later reinstated. The appellate court held (1) defendant's improper inclusion in the appellate record of certain materials that were not before the trial court was harmless and (2) based on (a) the historical view of bail revocation, (b) the fact the aim of the bail revocation statute was not crime prevention or punishment but a determination of when defendants should be free pending trial, and (c) an examination of the text of the bail revocation statute, including its lack of a scienter requirement, the temporary revocation of defendant's bail was not a criminal punishment and did not amount to jeopardy such that her motion to dismiss was correctly denied.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the trial court's denial of defendant's motion to dismiss the charges.

DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part, reversed in part, and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of unlawful possession of a firearm by a serious violent felon, in violation of Ind. Code § 35-47-4-5, after a jury trial in the Noble Circuit Court (Indiana). In a separate proceeding, the court found that defendant was an habitual offender. The court sentenced defendant to 20 years in prison on the primary offense and an additional 30 years as an habitual offender. Defendant appealed his conviction and sentence.


OVERVIEW: Armed with a warrant, police searched a home and found 18 firearms in a basement room where defendant and his girlfriend lived. Although the girlfriend claimed that the weapons were hers, a jury convicted defendant of possession of a firearm by a serious violent felon. OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed defendant's conviction, but reversed his sentence to the extent the trial court enhanced the sentence through the general offender statute, and remanded the case to the trial court with instructions to vacate the habitual sentence enhancement.

Lashley v. State

COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA, FIRST DISTRICT

March 26, 2001, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Transfer Denied June 14, 2001, Reported at: 2001 Ind. LEXIS 515.

DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted by the Morgan Superior Court (Indiana) of resisting law enforcement, as a Class A misdemeanor, and failure to identify self, a Class C misdemeanor. Defendant appealed the convictions, arguing that the traffic stop of his vehicle was unreasonable under Ind. Const. art. I, § 11, that the charging document was defective, and other errors.


OVERVIEW: The arresting officer was driving home when he observed appellant speeding. The officer was in his police cruiser, and was in uniform. When the officer stopped appellant for speeding, appellant got out of his car, approached the officer's vehicle yelling profanities, and demanding to know what the officer wanted. Appellant refused to show the officer his license and registration, claiming the officer lacked jurisdiction. Appellant got back in his car and drove away. The officer stopped appellant again and arrested him. The appellate court held the stop was reasonable. Ind. Code 34-28-5-3 stated that a law enforcement officer could, at any time, detain a person suspected of committing an infraction. There was no jurisdictional limitation on this authority of officers, including city police officers, to detain or stop individuals. Appellant admitted he was speeding when he was stopped. The court held the charging information was valid. The officer signed the information prior to the insertion of the charges against appellant. The appellate court held such signing was for administrative convenience. On its face the information satisfied Ind. Code § 35-34-1-2(b).


OUTCOME: The convictions were affirmed.

OVERVIEW: Plaintiff purchased approximately land in Warrick County on which he planned to construct eight buildings containing 457 mini-storage units. 755 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Brown v. State


No. 66A05-0007-CR-270


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA, FIFTH DISTRICT


744 N.E.2d 989; 2001 Ind. App. LEXIS 456



March 9, 2001, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] APPEAL FROM THE PULASKI SUPERIOR COURT. Cause No. 66D01-9905-CM-152. The Honorable Lisa M. Traylor-Wolff, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Following a fatal automobile accident, which was caused by the other driver, the Pulaski Superior Court (Indiana) convicted appellant of a class C misdemeanor for operating a vehicle while having marijuana in his body, a Schedule I controlled substance. Appellant filed an appeal challenging his conviction on the constitutional grounds of unreasonable search or seizure, equal protection and vagueness.


OVERVIEW: The appellate court found that the chemical testing of appellant's blood was a reasonable search, and that Ind. Code § 9-30-7 was constitutionally applied. Section 9-30-7-3 permitted the officer to have appellant's blood tested based solely on the ground that appellant had been in a fatal automobile accident, even though the other driver was at fault. Further, the statute did not remove the requirement for probable cause, it merely established appellant's consent, as a vehicle operator, following a fatal collision. Additionally, appellant's federal equal protection claim failed because the state had a legitimate purpose in providing for safe roadways and § 9-30-7 was rationally related to achieving that purpose. Appellant waived his state equal protection challenge as he failed to provide a separate analysis. Finally, Ind. Code § 9-30-5-1(b) was not unconstitutionally vague as it applied to the metabolites of marijuana. That section, read with Ind. Code § 35-48-2-4(b)(14), adequately and unambiguously informed persons of ordinary intelligence of the proscribed conduct.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed. The search was reasonable as the officer could draw blood based solely on the occurrence of the fatal automobile accident. There was no requirement that probable cause of intoxication be shown before appellant could be asked to submit to a chemical test. Further, appellant's federal equal protection challenge failed as the state had a legitimate purpose.

McBain v. Hamilton County


No. 29A05-0008-CV-343


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA, FIFTH DISTRICT


744 N.E.2d 984; 2001 Ind. App. LEXIS 457



March 9, 2001, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] APPEAL FROM THE HAMILTON SUPERIOR COURT. Cause No. 29D03-9709-MI-575. The Honorable William J. Hughes, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants challenged the decision of the Hamilton Superior Court (Indiana) which denied their petition to set aside tax sale.


OVERVIEW: Appellants were delinquent on their property taxes and the auditor's office sent them notice of tax sale which advised them that their property would be sold to recover the unpaid taxes pursuant to Ind. Code § 6-1.1-24-1-14. The notice was returned by the post office and the auditor's office did not forward the notice to the new address listed on the returned envelope. The property was then sold at a tax sale. The appellate court found that the adequacy of a county's notice of a tax sale was an important question that was likely to recur and the record of the tax sale of appellant's property could subject them to a negative collateral consequence that would be unjustified if the sale was invalid due to inadequate notice. The auditor's failure to re-mail the notice of the tax sale to appellant's new address deprived them of notice reasonably calculated under all the circumstances to apprise them of the pendency of the tax sale and failed to afford appellant's the opportunity to present their objections. The court held that as a result of the constitutionally inadequate notice, the tax sale was invalid and appellant's petition to set aside tax sale should have been granted.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court denying appellant's petition to set aside the tax sale of their property was reversed.

 

757 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


State v. Bilbrey


No. 18A04-0010-CR-433


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA, FOURTH DISTRICT


743 N.E.2d 796; 2001 Ind. App. LEXIS 370



March 6, 2001, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] APPEAL FROM THE DELAWARE CIRCUIT COURT NO. 2. Cause No. 18D02-9912-CF-94. The Honorable Richard A. Dailey, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: State sought review of a judgment of the Delaware Circuit Court (Indiana) which dismissed the information against defendant for operating a vehicle after suspension of license and while intoxicated.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's driver's license had been suspended for life, and the same was charged for operating a vehicle after such suspension, and for operating the vehicle while intoxicated. The trial court granted defendant's motion to dismiss, and the state appealed. On appeal, the court reversed. It held that the determination of whether defendant was operating the motor vehicle was a question of fact, and that the determination of such as a matter of law at the dismissal stage in the trial court was error.


OUTCOME: The decision of the trial court was reversed, because it was error to determine whether defendant was an operator as a matter of law.

 

758 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Spearman v. State


No. 49A04-0006-CR-261


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA, FOURTH DISTRICT


744 N.E.2d 545; 2001 Ind. App. LEXIS 368



March 6, 2001, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Appellant's Transfer Denied July 18, 2001, Reported at: 2001 Ind. LEXIS 649.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM THE MARION SUPERIOR COURT. Cause No. 49G04-9909-CF-168954. The Honorable Diane Marger Moore, Master Commissioner.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Marion Superior Court in which he was convicted of unlawful possession of a firearm by a serious violent felon.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed his conviction for unlawful possession of a firearm by a serious violent felon, contending that the trial court violated his rights to due process under U.S. Const. amend. XIV when it denied his motion to bifurcate portions of the proceedings. Defendant moved for bifurcated proceedings so that the jury would not be told of his criminal confinement conviction before it determined whether he was in possession of a firearm. On appeal, the court affirmed. It held that evidence of the felony was properly before the jury because it was an element of the crime, and the prejudicial effect could have been mitigated by stipulation.


OUTCOME: Judgment was affirmed, because the prior conviction was a necessary element of the crime, and prejudice could have been mitigated by stipulation.

 

759 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


City of Anderson v. Davis


No. 30A01-0002-CV-51


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA, FIRST DISTRICT


743 N.E.2d 359; 2001 Ind. App. LEXIS 279



February 21, 2001, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Appellee's Transfer Denied July 18, 2001, Reported at: 2001 Ind. LEXIS 631.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM THE HANCOCK SUPERIOR COURT. Cause No. 30D02-9809-CP-291. The Honorable Richard D. Culver, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff police officer sued defendants, city and police dog handler, for personal injuries. The Hancock Superior Court (Indiana) found for plaintiff and awarded damages. Defendants appealed claiming immunity from liability under the Indiana Tort Claims Act (ITCA), Ind. Code §§ 34-13-3-1 to -25.


OVERVIEW: After a juvenile escaped from a detention center, dogs were brought in to search for him. During the search, plaintiff was bitten by one of the dogs. The court held that because plaintiff's damages resulted from defendant police dog handler's deployment of the dog, which constituted the enforcement of law in the scope of his employment, defendants were immune from liability pursuant to the ITCA. Indiana governmental units were afforded extensive protections from tort liability under the ITCA. The only exceptions to immunity specifically recognized in the ITCA were false arrest and false imprisonment. Ind. Code § 34-13-3-3(7). Defendant officer was defendant city's employee at the time of the incident, plaintiff did not allege false arrest or false imprisonment, and plaintiff did not contend that defendant officer was acting outside the scope of his employment when he deployed the dog. The use of the dog amounted to the enforcement of the law within the meaning of the ITCA. A prior federal court action had already determined that the actions did not amount to excessive force.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed. Defendants had statutory immunity from liability for plaintiff's injuries because the deployment of the dog was a law enforcement activity within the scope of defendant dog handler's employment. Collateral estoppel barred a determination of whether defendant dog handler used excessive force.

 

760 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Kopkey v. State


No. 66A04-0005-CR-220


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA, FOURTH DISTRICT


743 N.E.2d 331; 2001 Ind. App. LEXIS 77



January 29, 2001, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Transfer denied by Kopkey v. State, 753 N.E.2d 11, 2001 Ind. LEXIS 382 (Ind., 2001)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] APPEAL FROM THE PULASKI CIRCUIT COURT. Cause No. 66C01-9609-CF-32. The Honorable Robert W. Thacker, Special Judge.
Kopkey v. State, 725 N.E.2d 535, 2000 Ind. App. LEXIS 291 (Ind. Ct. App., 2000)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed Pulaski Circuit Court (Indiana) order revoking his in-home detention and revocation and ordering his incarceration, arguing that the trial court should have suppressed drug test results, that probation should not have been revoked before it began, and that sentencing order was impermissibly unclear.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's plea agreement resulted in a sentence with work release, at-home detention, and probation elements. Detention and probation were revoked when defendant flunked drug tests authorized by terms of his at-home detention. The court looked to the extent of a detainee's privacy interest, the extent of actual intrusion, and the governmental interest at stake, and found that the urine tests were reasonable and their results admissible. Defendant's probationary period started at sentencing, even though actual probation was revoked before it began, so the trial court had the power to revoke it prospectively for violation within the probationary period. Finally, although the sentencing order confusingly failed to indicate whether in-home detention was a direct community corrections placement or a condition of probation, when read together with the plea agreement, which was construed as any other contract, the parties clearly intended that defendant should be subject to incarceration for violation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order. The drug test was a reasonable intrusion under the circumstances. Prospective revocation of probation was proper because defendant's probationary period had already begun. Finally, when the sentencing order and defendant's plea agreement were read together, intent that violation result in incarceration was clear.

 

761 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Shultz v. State


No. 25A03-0003-CR-106


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA, THIRD DISTRICT


742 N.E.2d 961; 2001 Ind. App. LEXIS 11



January 11, 2001, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Transfer Denied June 20, 2001, Reported at: 2001 Ind. LEXIS 551.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM THE FULTON CIRCUIT COURT. Cause No.25C01-9711-DF-82. The Honorable Douglas B. Morton, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part and reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Criminal appellant challenged his conviction following a bench trial on three counts of receiving stolen property, entered by the Fulton Circuit Court (Indiana), where he was charged with five counts of receiving stolen property and six counts of receiving stolen auto parts.


OVERVIEW: Criminal appellant was charged with five counts of receiving stolen property and six counts of receiving stolen auto parts. Eight of the 11 counts were dismissed. Police went to appellant's residence after receiving information he might know about stolen property. Officers saw a white semi-tractor, which resembled one that had been stolen, sitting in or near the driveway. Although the semi-tractor appeared to be the one that had been stolen, the officers looked for a partial vehicle identification number (VIN) in the wheel-well. An officer using a flashlight had to wipe dirt off the VIN plate to observe its numbers. Officers also walked into outbuildings where they observed a trenching machine. Further, while at appellant's back door, they saw a gold GMC Jimmy vehicle which had its VIN concealed. Appellant's motion to suppress each item was denied. He appealed. The court partly affirmed and partly reversed. Wiping the dirt off the semi-tractor's VIN plate and walking into the building to view the trenching machine violated appellant's reasonable expectation of privacy, but viewing the gold GMC Jimmy from the back door while it was parked in the carport did not.


OUTCOME: Judgment partly affirmed and partly reversed because police officers' observation of vehicle in carport, a gold GMC Jimmy missing its license plate and containing a concealed vehicle identification number (VIN), did not involve a search and did not require that evidence be suppressed, but knocking dirt off semi-tractor's VIN and walking into outbuildings to view a trenching machine involved unreasonable searches requiring suppression.

Hatchett v. State


No. 49A05-0007-CR-295


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA, FIFTH DISTRICT


740 N.E.2d 920; 2000 Ind. App. LEXIS 2054



December 20, 2000, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Transfer denied by Hatchett v. State, 753 N.E.2d 8, 2001 Ind. LEXIS 266 (Ind., 2001)
Post-conviction relief denied at Hatchett v. State, 2003 Ind. App. LEXIS 1595 (Ind. Ct. App., Aug. 29, 2003)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] APPEAL FROM THE MARION SUPERIOR COURT, CRIMINAL DIVISION 6. Cause No. 49G06-9909-CF-151178. The Honorable Alex R. Murphy, Master Commissioner.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part and remanded in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed convictions in Marion Superior Court (Indiana) of unlawful firearm possession by a serious violent felon and carrying a handgun without a license, arguing entitlement to bifurcated trial, that punishment was disproportionate, violations of Ind. Const. art. I, §§ 14, 23, and improper reliance on aggravating factors in sentencing.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of carrying a firearm without a license and, because of an old robbery conviction, handgun possession by a serious violent felon. The court held that defendant waived appeal of failure to bifurcate by not objecting before trial. The Indiana legislature was free to determine which offenses warranted which sentences. Equal protection was not violated by the legislative determination, not mathematically precise, that some crimes were violent serious felonies for purposes of the firearms law and some were not. However, the fact that the same evidence was probably used to convict defendant of both offenses violated double jeopardy protection and required vacation of one conviction. Finally, the trial court erred in applying defendant's need for corrective activity relating to alcoholism and danger of minimizing his offense's seriousness as aggravating factors, so remand was required to determine whether the single aggravating factor of criminal history warranted his sentence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the serious violent felon conviction, but vacated the other conviction for double jeopardy violation. Because the trial court improperly applied the need for corrective activity and depreciating the seriousness of the offense aggravators, the court remanded for determination whether the single remaining aggravator justified the sentence.
